Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
22-01-2023
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
Motivation: Monitoring patients affected by epilepsy through the use of electroencephalog-
raphy (EEG) is widely used at hospitals for pre-surgical evaluation. The process of EEG
monitoring takes several days, which results in a vast amount of information to be pro-
cessed. In the clinical practice, evaluation of seizures is done by visual inspection, which
is time consuming and often subjective. An automatic seizure detection technique would
therefore support visual analysis and drastically decrease the workload of clinicians.
Purpose: The main objective of this project is to research methods that are applicable
for seizure detection based on EEG signals. A patient specific method that is suitable
for onset detection is to be implemented and the feasibility of a seizure detector is to be
determined.
Data: The data that is used is the publicly available CHB-MIT Scalp EEG Database col-
lected at the Children’s Hospital Boston. This database consists of 24 cases out of which
22 are used. 18 EEG channels that are arranged according to the bipolar montage are
used across all cases.
Methods: A literature review is conducted, where relevant publications are identified.
Based on the literature review, the scope of the thesis is narrowed down to focus only on
patient-specific onset detection through the use of the CHB-MIT Scalp EEG Database.
The methods proposed in two of the publications are partly replicated, implying that ele-
ments of both publications are used.
To extract features, the harmonic wavelet packet transform is applied. The coefficients
corresponding to certain frequency bands are obtained and the energy of the coefficients
is used as features. The features are combined to form a feature vector, which is struc-
tured such that spectral, temporal and spatial properties of EEG are encoded.
Classification is performed using a leave-one-out scheme, where a support vector ma-
chine in combination with a Gaussian kernel is applied. A patient specific classifier is
trained for every single seizure that is available for the corresponding patient. The seizure
of interest is left out of the training process and the remaining seizures belonging to the pa-
tient along with separate non-seizure data are used as training data. The EEG recording
containing the left out seizure is then used for testing. The results of the testing process
are used to compute the performance in terms of sensitivity, detection delay and hourly
false alarm rate. This process is completed for every seizure and every patient.
Results: A working method for subject specific seizure onset detection is implemented.
The onsets of 129 out of 143 seizures are able to be correctly detected resulting in a
sensitivity of 90%. The average detection delay across all seizures and patients is 7.6
seconds, the median detection delay across all seizures and patients is 6 seconds, the
average hourly false alarm rate across all seizures and patients is 11.21 per hour and the
median hourly false alarm rate across all seizures and patients is 0 per hour.
Conclusion: This project shows that it is feasible to detect the onset of seizures and
that seizure detection methods potentially can be used to support visual analysis of EEG
recordings performed by healthcare professionals or potentially be used in scenarios
where a quick reaction to a seizure is needed.
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning

4.3
Implementation of the harmonic wavelet transform [18].
The input sig-
nal x(t) is first Fourier transformed and then multiplied with the harmonic
wavelet at scale m, n in the frequency domain resulting in frequency domain
wavelet coefficients. The coefficients are then inverse Fourier transformed
resulting in time domain wavelet coefficients. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 32
4.4
Spectral feature extraction method [4]. An epoch of size L from EEG chan-
nel k is passed to the wavelet packet transform spanning the range 0-32
Hz. The energy in each band Sm is computed to form the feature vector x
consisting of M elements. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 33
4.5
Spatial feature extraction method [4]. The feature vectors x from each
channel are concatenated to form the feature vector XT of size M · K for
an epoch of size L. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 33
4.6
Inclusion of time evolution for the feature vectors [4]. Three non-overlapping
feature vectors XT are concatenated to form the feature vector χT of size
M · K · W. The first feature vector χT is formed after 6 seconds and after
that a new one is formed every second. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 34
4.7
Architecture of seizure detector used for testing [4]. The first classification
is performed after 6 seconds and then every second until the end of the
signal has been reached. The first 5 elements of the output vector are
initialized with zeros because no classification is performed during the first
5 seconds.
. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . 36

==PAGE:15==
1
Introduction
In this chapter the motivation, a short introduction to related work and the problem state-
ment of the thesis are introduced. Furthermore an outline of the thesis is presented.
1.1
Motivation
Epilepsy is a common chronic disorder of the central nervous system affecting approxi-
mately 1% of the world’s population. The factors that give rise to epilepsy are not clearly
known. Common causes can be divided into genetic and acquired causes. Some of the
acquired causes include brain trauma, stroke and tumours [1]. Among children and young
people, genetics are often the reason for epilepsy, whereas it is more common for adults
and elderly people that acquired reasons are to blame [2].
Individuals affected by epilepsy experience recurrent seizures, which are sudden anoma-
lies in the electrical activity of the brain. These anomalies produce disturbing symptoms,
which range between inattention to loss of control and consciousness due to full body
convulsions. The unpredictable occurrences of seizures can affect the cognitive, psycho-
logical and social aspects of individuals and reduce the quality of life greatly. Seizures
occur at different rates, which often is multiple times a day, daily or weekly. Due to loss
of control or consciousness, serious injuries can occur, which pose a great risk for people
affected by epilepsy.
An approach to mitigate the risk of injuries is the ability to detect the onset of a seizure [2].
This is especially suitable for hospitals, where long term recordings of electrical brain ac-
tivity in the form of electroencephalography (EEG) is recorded. Currently, EEG monitoring
of epilepsy is the golden standard at hospitals for pre-surgical evaluation [3]. EEG is a
non-invasive recording of brain activity that has a high temporal resolution, which makes
it convenient to accurately detect the onset of seizures. As EEG monitoring takes several
days, the amount of information to be processed is vast. In the clinical practice, evaluation
of seizures is done by visual inspection, which is time consuming and often subjective.
An automatic seizure detection technique would therefore support visual analysis and
drastically decrease the workload of clinicians.
For localization of the brain region where the seizure originates, known as the epilepto-
genic focus, a single-photon emission computerized tomography (SPECT) scan is used
[2]. During the onset of a seizure, the blood flow at the epileptogenic focus is altered,
which is highlighted by the SPECT scan. The scan needs to be initiated quickly after the
electrographic onset of the seizure to accurately localize the focus. Therefore the auto-
matic seizure detection technique not only needs to be accurate, but also needs to be
able to detect the onset of seizures with a minimal delay.
1.2
Related work
The electrical activity of the brain is measurable through the use of scalp EEG. During
epileptic seizures the characteristics of this activity change and irregularities can be de-
tected. The activity of the brain, however, consists of different classes whose character-
istics often overlap. Therefore the challenge is to identify and extract essential features
that are able to separate seizure activity from non-seizure activity. Another challenge is
that the electrical activity of the brain during a seizure is patient-specific. The character-
istics of an EEG waveform of one individual during a seizure is not necessarily similar to
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
1
==PAGE:16==
the characteristics of another individual during a seizure. Furthermore seizures can be
categorized into different types with different characteristics.
Prior works generally make use of publicly available EEG recordings. The general process
of handling these recordings consists of feature extraction, classification and evaluation.
In some cases a pre-processing step and a feature selection method are included, which
is a method to reduce or eliminate redundant features. Prior works propose features to
extract, but not necessarily why these features are suitable for seizure detection. Another
large aspect of seizure detection is channel selection.
Many of the works make use of different features, classifiers, performance metrics and
datasets. This makes a direct comparison between the different methods difficult. Whether
the method is patient specific or non-patient specific also plays a role. Depending on the
application, a good performance metric is the sensitivity as this is a measure for how well
the method is able to detect all the seizures. Metrics such as a high specificity and a
low false alarm rate are good for reducing the workload of the clinicians as the amount of
time spend on rejecting false detections is reduced. The latency can also be an important
metric, for example in the case of SPECT scan initiation.
To narrow down the amount related work and to identify state of the art methods for seizure
onset detection, the following criteria are considered:
• That the CHB-MIT Scalp EEG Database [4][5] is used.
• That the detection method is subject specific and based on onset detection.
• That the performance evaluation of the presented method is based on sensitivity,
false alarm rate per hour and detection latency.
The related works considered are further discussed in Chapter 3.
1.3
Problem statement
The focus of this thesis is to investigate methods for epileptic seizure detection based
on scalp EEG. This includes proof-of-principle of a selected method from the literature
and based on the findings, determine the feasibility of a seizure detector. To successfully
achieve this, the following sub-goals are defined:
• To perform a literature study of epileptic seizure detection methods based on EEG
technology, provide an overview of the research field, and develop an understanding
of the possibilities and limitations with regards to seizure detection.
• To analyze the provided publicly available data.
• To replicate a method from the literature as proof-of-principle based on the literature
study and if possible improve upon the selected method.
• To evaluate the real time performance of the implemented method.
1.4

content.

2
Background
This chapter describes how epilepsy is defined and some common causes for the disor-
der. A general description of epileptic seizures is also included. Furthermore, this chap-
ter covers EEG and its application to epilepsy, which includes the different processes of
recording EEG and how brain activity is characterized with regards to EEG. Lastly the
theory behind the wavelet transform and support vector machines is covered including
the definition of performance metrics specific to this thesis.
2.1
Epilepsy
Epilepsy is a chronic neurological disorder affecting approximately 65 million people world-
wide [6]. The International League against Epilepsy (ILAE) defines epilepsy as ”a disor-
der of the brain characterized by an enduring predisposition to generate epileptic seizures
and by the neurobiologic, cognitive, psychological, and social consequences of this con-
dition” [7]. In other words, epilepsy results in brain activity becoming abnormal, which
causes seizures. The causes that give rise to the disorder still remain largely unknown,
but epilepsy is most common among people where the brain has been affected by a dis-
turbance, which for example could be head trauma. For children and young people some
common causes are genetic disorders, congenital anomalies and birth trauma. For adults
and elderly people some causes are strokes and tumors [2]. The lives of people affected
by epilepsy are influenced greatly because of the unpredictability of the disorder and the
loss of control that comes with it. This applies to both social activities and other aspects
of daily living.
2.2
Seizures
An epileptic seizure is a transient abnormal synchronisation of neurons in the brain. The
International League against Epilepsy (ILAE) defines an epileptic seizure as ”a transient
occurrence of signs and/or symptoms due to abnormal excessive or synchronous neu-
ronal activity in the brain” [7]. Seizures are either categorised as generalized, focal or
epileptic spasms. The different types of seizures are shown in Table 2.1 and an illustra-
tion of the area of effect of generalized and focal seizures is depicted in Figure 2.1.
Table 2.1: Types and sub-types of epileptic seizures. Seizures are categorized as gener-
alized seizures, focal seizures or epileptic spasms [8].
Seizure types
Generalized seizures
- Tonic-clonic
- Absence
- Myoclonic
- Atonic
Focal seizures
Epileptic spasms
2.2.1
Focal seizures
A focal seizure arises and remains in one part of the brain. If the consciousness of an
individual is not affected by a seizure, the seizure is called a focal seizure without loss of
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
5
==PAGE:20==
Figure 2.1: Area of effect of generalized and focal seizures. Generalized seizures affect
the whole brain, whereas focal seizures only affect one part of the brain [9].
consciousness or a simple focal seizure. Seizures that result in a loss of consciousness or
awareness are called focal seizures with impaired awareness or complex focal seizures.
Simple focal seizures do not affect the consciousness, but are able to affect the so-
matosensory and autonomic systems of an individual depending on which part of the
cortex is affected. Somatosensory symptoms include altering of senses, such as smell,
feel, taste, sound and sight. An autonomic symptom is involuntary jerking of a body part.
Individuals experiencing a focal seizure without loss of consciousness may also be af-
fected emotionally. Seizures of this type usually last less than a minute and individuals
are able to recall the event [2].
Complex focal seizures are seizures during which individuals are unable to respond nor-
mally. They are often associated with the temporal lobe. Complex focal seizures can be
preceded by an aura, which is a simple focal seizure with sensory or autonomic symptoms
where consciousness is retained. Auras can precede complex focal seizures by seconds
or even minutes. Complex focal seizures usually last 1-3 minutes, followed by a period
of confusion lasting several minutes [2].
2.2.2
Generalized seizures
A generalized seizure involves the whole brain. If the clinical manifestations are spastic
muscle activity, the seizure is a generalized convulsive seizure. Otherwise the seizures
are generalized non-convulsive seizures. Generalized convulsive seizures can be further
divided into the subtypes myoclonic, clonic, tonic and tonic-clonic seizures. For general-
ized non-convulsive seizures the subtypes are called absence and atonic seizures [8].
As mentioned above, generalized convulsive seizures can be further divided into sub-
types based on the characteristics of the involuntary muscular activity and the state of
consciousness of an individual. Myoclonic seizures lead to quick alteration of muscular
contraction and relaxation, which can either affect one or both sides of the body. The
consciousness of an individual is not necessarily altered. Clonic seizures are similar to
myoclonic seizures but with a slower alteration of muscular contraction and relaxation.
Twitching and jerking of muscles is associated with clonic seizures. Individuals affected
6
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:21==
by clonic seizures may lose consciousness. Tonic seizures result in contraction or stiff-
ening of truncal and facial muscles. Flexion of upper extremities and extension of lower
extremities can also occur. Due to the arms, legs and back being affected, this type of
seizure often leads to that individuals fall or collapse. Tonic-clonic seizures are a com-
bination of tonic and clonic seizures with clinical manifestations of both seizure types.
Tonic-clonic seizures can cause immediate loss of consciousness and they begin with-
out a warning. The duration of a tonic-clonic seizure is approximately between one and
two minutes, but consciousness can be lost for 10 to 15 minutes and individuals may
experience fatigue for several hours or days after the event [2].
Generalized non-convulsive seizures are divided into absence and atonic seizures. Ab-
sence seizures are characterised by loss of consciousness or awareness. Manifestations
of this type of seizure are eye blinking, staring and other minor body movements. Seizures
of this type have a short duration of around 5-60 seconds, but they occur many times daily
[2]. Atonic seizures do not lead to loss of consciousness but lead to loss of muscle control,
which often leads individuals to fall or collapse resulting in injuries.
2.2.3
Epileptic spasms
Epileptic spasms are characterized by extension or flexion of extremities occurring for a
short duration but recurring in clusters lasting several minutes [8]. The origin of epileptic
spasms is unknown but they can occur at any age.
2.3
Status epilepticus
Status epilepticus is a condition that occurs when an individual is experiencing continu-
ous seizures lasting more than five minutes or if seizures are occurring frequently for a
prolonged duration such that they essentially can be considered continuous [2]. The ac-
tivity during status epilepticus can be either convulsive or non-convulsive. In the convul-
sive case, individuals experience tonic-clonic seizures without regaining consciousness,
whereas individuals in the other case appear to be in a state of coma. Individuals with
status epilepticus have an increased chance of receiving permanent brain damage.
2.4
Treatment of epilepsy
Epilepsy can be treated in different ways. Approximately 70% of newly diagnosed people
living with epilepsy can be treated by antiepileptic drugs [8]. These drugs decrease the
electrical activity of the brain or limit the neurons’ capacity to fire such that certain seizures
do not occur. Individuals that are not able to be treated effectively by medications have
to be treated in other ways.
Surgery is another treatment option. There are different types of surgery depending on the
origin of the seizure and the age of the individual. Epilepsy surgery consists of resective
and functional surgery. The goal of resective surgery is to remove the epileptogenic focus
such that seizures no longer occur. Types of resective surgery are anterior temporal lobe
resection, lesionectomy, neocortical resections and hemispherectomy [10]. Functional
surgery is another type of surgery that is not curative but instead seeks to relieve seizure
symptoms. Types of functional surgery include corpus callosotomy, multiple subpial tran-
section and stimulation, such as vagal nerve stimulation or deep brain stimulation [10].
To be considered for surgery, a profound evaluation by medical specialists must be per-
formed. Surgery is also only viable if the part of the brain in which the seizure originates
is able to be determined accurately. To do this, clinical, electrographic, anatomical, func-
tional and metabolic evidence must be collected. All of this is done using video monitoring,
EEG monitoring, magnetic resonance imaging, neurophysical testing, positron emission
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
7
==PAGE:22==
tomography (PET) scans and single photon emission computerized tomography (SPECT)
scans.
2.5
Electroencephalography
As mentioned in the previous section, EEG plays a large role in evaluating if an individual
can undergo surgery for treatment of epilepsy, but it is also a diagnostic procedure used
earlier in the process to confirm the presence of the disorder. Electroencephalography is
a noninvasive method for recording the electrical activity of the brain by placing electrodes
on the scalp. Action potentials from neurons cause the release of neurotransmitters at
the synapses. This in turn leads to postsynaptic potentials in the dendrites of the neu-
rons receiving the signal [11]. The sum of postsynaptic potentials from several thousand
neurons is reflected in the EEG. Since EEG is non-invasive, the method is largely only
capturing electrical activity in the cerebral cortex and hence currents stemming from deep
within the brain are not captured. EEG has a low spatial resolution due to the electrodes
not being directly in contact with the brain, whereas the temporal resolution is high [11].
2.5.1
EEG recording
The recording of EEG involves a subject wearing an electrode cap. Electrode locations
on the scalp are specified by the international 10-20 system as shown in Figure 2.2. This
system is a convention used to standardize the locations of the electrodes on the scalp
[11]. The basis for the placement of the electrodes is the distance between nasion and
inion, where the distance between two electrodes is either 10% or 20% of the distance
between nasion and inion.
Figure 2.2: International 10-20 system for standardized electrode placement [2]. This
system is used throughout the thesis.
EEG recordings for visual review can have either a referential or bipolar montage [2]. In
the bipolar montage, the voltage of each electrode is compared to an adjacent one forming
a chain of electrodes. There are different types of bipolar montages. A common one is the
8
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:23==
longitudinal montage where vertical electrode chains are formed starting from the nasion
and ending at the inion [12]. This type of montage is shown in Figure 2.3. Considering
the electrodes in the figure, the right temporal chain is formed by linking Fp2 to F8 and F8
is then linked to T4. This continues until arriving at O2 such that the right temporal chain
consists of Fp2-F8, F8-T4, T4-T6 and T6-O2. In the right parasagittal chain Fp2 is linked
to F4 continuing until O2. The same procedure forms two chains in the left hemisphere
and the central chain is made up of Fz, Cz and Pz. Other types of bipolar montages
are the circumferential montage, where electrodes are linked in circular chains, and the
transverse montage, where electrodes are not linked vertically from nasion to inion but
rather horizontally from ear to ear.
Figure 2.3: Longitudinal bipolar montage. An electrode at the tip of an arrow is subtracted
from the electrode at the tail of the arrow.
In the referential montage each electrode is compared to a reference electrode, which in
Figure 2.2 is either A1 or A2 located at the mastoids behind the ears. Electrodes from
the right hemisphere are usually compared to the left reference electrode A1, whereas
electrodes from the left hemisphere are compared to the right reference A2. This is done
to ensure that the reference electrode is not greatly affected by brain activity in the hemi-
sphere of interest [2].
Both referential and bipolar montages have advantages and disadvantages. A main ad-
vantage of referential montages is that absolute potentials of electrodes are observed
instead of differences, resulting in clearly observable changes. If the reference is noisy
or otherwise contaminated by artifacts, the activity recorded by the active electrodes be-
comes hard to interpret. Bipolar montages do not suffer from common-mode noise, be-
cause each active electrode is subtracted from an adjacent one. This however leads to
attenuation of recorded activity [2]. Generally referential montages are the best option for
generalized activity whereas bipolar montages are better suited for focal activity [12].
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
9
==PAGE:24==
2.5.2
EEG activity
The activity of the brain recorded by EEG can be characterized by different variables.
These variables are fundamental frequency, amplitude, morphology, localization and re-
activity, which are all associated with an individual’s age and state of consciousness [2].
The fundamental frequency is a measure of the rate at which the EEG waveform is re-
peated over a one second period. Fundamental frequencies are associated with rhythmic
or arrhythmic EEG waveforms, where an EEG waveform with a constant or stable fun-
damental frequency is rhythmic. Otherwise the waveform is said to be arrhythmic. The
amplitude refers to the peak voltage of an EEG waveform typically in the unit of microvolts.
A sudden reduction in amplitude of an EEG waveform is referred to as suppression or de-
pression. The morphology of an EEG waveform is a description of the shape based on the
fundamental frequency and the amplitude of all components of the waveform. Morpholo-
gies are either monomorphic or polymorphic, where an EEG waveform that consists of a
single component is considered monomorphic and waveforms composed of multiple com-
ponents are considered polymorphic. A combination of multiple waveforms with different
morphologies is known as a complex. Localization refers to the location of the activity
with regards to the different regions of the head as shown in Figure 2.4. If activity is only
observed in a certain region, it is referred to as focal activity, whereas it is generalized if it
is observed in all regions. Symmetric activity is if the activity exhibits equal fundamental
frequency, amplitude and morphology in both sides of the head. The reactivity of an EEG
waveform describes the change in fundamental frequency, amplitude, morphology and
localization based on a stimulus.
Figure 2.4: Clinical terms for the different regions of the head [2].
Normal electrical activity of the brain consists of different waves at distinct frequency
ranges. These brain waves or rhythms are divided into delta, theta, alpha, beta and
gamma rhythms, of which the first four a sketch is displayed in Figure 2.5. The frequency
bands of the different rhythms are defined as [11]:
• Delta (δ): 0.5 - 4 Hz
10
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:25==
• Theta (θ): 4 - 8 Hz
• Alpha (α): 8 - 13 Hz
• Beta (β): 13 - 30 Hz
• Gamma (γ): 30 - 100 Hz
The delta rhythm has a frequency below 4 Hz and an amplitude that is larger than the
amplitude of the other rhythms. This rhythm is observed in in infants and sleeping adults.
For adults the rhythm is mainly present frontally, whereas it is observed posteriorly in
infants [2].
The theta rhythm exhibits a frequency between 4 and 8 Hz and is observable in children
and sleeping adults. This type of activity is mostly observed in the left region of the head,
especially in the temporal, parietal and central regions and hence it is asymmetric [2].
The alpha rhythm has a frequency between 8 and 13 Hz and is observed in the occipital
region of the head. This rhythm is visible when an adult closes the eyes and relaxes. Once
the eyes are opened, the alpha rhythm is suppressed which is visible by a decrease in
amplitude [11].
The beta rhythm exhibits a frequency between 13 and 30 Hz and is observed in the frontal
and parietal regions. This rhythm is associated with alertness or active concentration [11],
and is suppressed by voluntary movement [2].
The gamma rhythm, involving the frequency range 30 to 100 Hz, is associated with tasks
involving short term memory and multisensory integration, which refers to the reaction of
the brain when receiving multiple sensory stimulations [11].
Beta 13-30 Hz
Alpha 8-13 Hz
Theta 4-8 Hz
Delta 0.5-4 Hz
Figure 2.5: EEG rhythms and corresponding frequency ranges. This figure is inspired by
[11] and only serves as a sketch as it is not based on real EEG data.
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning

==PAGE:26==
2.5.3
Abnormal EEG activity
Abnormal EEG activity manifests as unusual waveforms and deviates from normal EEG
activity in terms of frequency, amplitude, morphology, localization and reactivity. Wave-
forms that are considered abnormal include spike and sharp waves, periodic discharges,
rhythmic hypersynchrony and electrocerebral inactivity. Most of these abnormalities ap-
pear in individuals with epilepsy during the interictal periods, which is the period between
seizures. The characteristics of an individual’s EEG during a seizure is distinguishable
from the same individual’s normal EEG activity in terms of morphology, but it can however
resemble normal EEG activity from another individual [2]. Hence it is difficult to general-
ize seizures, because the electrographic characteristics of seizures are unique for each
individual.
2.5.4
Seizure EEG
Seizures are visible in the scalp EEG due to sudden changes in the spectral energy of
certain EEG channels. This redistribution of energy is caused by hypersynchrony, which
is a synchronization of activity in a group of neurons [13]. The synchronization leads to
changes in the EEG as frequency components in the 0-25 Hz range appear or disap-
pear. Frequency components affected vary from individual to individual and the channels
affected are also specific for individuals [4].
A seizure from an individual in the CHB-MIT database is displayed in Figure 2.6. The
seizure starts at 2996 seconds and is first characterized by a slight increase in amplitude
and later on an even larger increase. In the latter part of the figure, an increase in fre-
quency is visible in some of the channels. The scalp EEG of another individual during a
seizure is shown in Figure 2.7, which at the first glance might look similar to the scalp EEG
of the first individual during a seizure, but the electrographic characteristics are different
for the two individuals. For the first individual, the waveform changes immediately after
seizure onset with a slight increase in amplitude. When advancing further into the seizure
at around the 3000 second mark, an even larger increase in amplitude and frequency is
visible in some of the channels. For the second individual, the amplitude also increases
after seizure onset, whereas the frequency decreases slightly in some of the channels.
An increase in frequency is first visible some time after the onset of the seizure. Further-
more a difference in localization is visible. The seizure mostly affects the frontal channels
of the first individual, whereas the seizure activity is prominent in the parietal channels of
the second individual.
To further illustrate the change in frequency, the frequency spectrum of a single channel
is analyzed. For the first individual this is done for the channel F8-T8 and for the second
individual this is done for the channel C4-P4. A 2 second window of normal activity is
extracted and visualized in the frequency domain. Furthermore 2 second windows are
extracted immediately after onset and 6 seconds after onset. This is visualized in Figures
2.8 and 2.9. It can be seen that the frequency content changes after seizure onset. For
the first individual the change is small immediately after onset, whereas the frequency
content increases when advancing further into the seizure. For the second individual
the change is large in the 0-7 Hz range immediately after onset, whereas the change in
the higher frequency range is most prominent when advancing further into the seizure.
Nevertheless, a change in frequency content is observable for both individuals, but the
frequency bands affected by the seizure, are different from individual to individual.

2.6
Wavelet transform
In Section 2.5 it was seen that the frequency content in certain EEG channels changes
during a seizure. This change in frequency content can be used to distinguish seizure
activity from normal activity. A method to analyze frequency content is the wavelet trans-
form, which is the topic of this section. The wavelet transform overcomes some of the
challenges that the short time Fourier transform faces. In the short time Fourier trans-
form, windows of equal size are analyzed. A drawback of this is that properties or features
that have a longer duration than the window, will not be covered to full extent. Another
problem is the time-frequency resolution. A large window will result in a good frequency
resolution but in a bad time resolution, whereas a narrow window will result in a good time
resolution, but in bad frequency resolution. The wavelet transform solves the problem of
time-frequency resolution by applying a wavelet function instead of a window [14].
2.6.1
Continuous wavelet transform
The definition of the continuous wavelet transform is [15]:
γ(s, τ) =

(2.2)
In Equation 2.2, ψ(t) is the wavelet function, which is also referred to as the mother
wavelet. The wavelet function can be shifted and scaled based on the shift factor τ and
the scale factor s. Scale values of s < 1 result in a compressed wavelet function, whereas
values of s > 1 result in a stretched wavelet function. The factor
1
√s is used for energy
normalization [16]. A wavelet basis function can be specified depending on the properties
of the signal that is to be transformed. For a function to be considered as a wavelet basis
function, it needs to fulfill certain properties. Two of the most important properties are the
admissibility and the regularity conditions. In summary, the admissibility condition speci-
fies that the wavelet function ψ(t) must have a spectrum resembling a band-pass, which
also implies that the wavelet in the time domain must be a wave with an average value of
zero. The regularity condition specifies that the wavelet function must have a fast decay
[16]. Functions that abide these properties can be considered as wavelets.
2.6.2
Discrete wavelet transform
In the continuous wavelet transform, translation and scale parameters of the wavelets
vary continuously, which is not applicable in practice. The discrete wavelet transform,
on the other hand, allows the wavelets to be discretely scaled and translated, resulting
in a reduction of redundant components. The discrete wavelet transform is a result of
modifying Equation 2.2 with s = sj

2.6.3
Wavelet packet transform
The implementation of the discrete wavelet transform illustrated in Figure 2.10 only de-
composes approximation coefficients, resulting in a low resolution in the high frequency
region [18]. To overcome this, the wavelet packet transform can be applied. The wavelet
packet transform decomposes both approximation and detail coefficients, resulting in 2j
groups of coefficients. The wavelet packet transform is illustrated in Figure 2.11.

(2.9)
Based on the expression in Equation 2.9, the harmonic wavelet transform is defined as

The harmonic wavelet has a compact frequency expression and hence the harmonic
wavelet transform can be obtained through Fourier transform and inverse Fourier trans-
form operations [18]. Therefore, the harmonic wavelet transform in the frequency domain
can be expressed as:
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
17
==PAGE:32==
HWT(m, n, f) = X(f)Ψ∗((n −m)f)
(2.11)
Here X(f) is the Fourier transform of the signal x(t) and Ψ∗((n −m)f) is the complex
conjugate of the Fourier transform of the harmonic wavelet at scale (m, n).
2.6.5
Harmonic wavelet packet transform
As mentioned in Equation 2.6, the scale parameters m and n determine the bandwidth
of the harmonic wavelet. The scale parameters can be varied such that the bandwidth
fits a certain frequency range. In the harmonic wavelet packet transform, the number
of subbands is given by 2j, where j is the decomposition level of the harmonic wavelet
transform. This means that the bandwidth can be expressed based on the decomposition
level j and the highest frequency component fh of the signal that is to be analyzed:
fb = fh
2j
(2.12)
The harmonic wavelet packet coefficients can thus be expressed as [18]:
hwpt(j, l, k) = hwt(m, n, k)
(2.13)
Here hwpt(j, l, k) are the harmonic wavelet packet coefficients of the lth subband in the
decomposition level j. To achieve this, the scale parameters m and n need to be selected
such that:
m = l · fh
2j = l · fb,
l = 0, 1, ..., 2j −1
(2.14)
n = (l + 1) · fh
2j = (l + 1) · fb,
l = 0, 1, ..., 2j −1
(2.15)
Based on this, bandwidths can be defined to fit certain frequency ranges.
2.7
Support vector machines
Support vector machines (SVM) are widely used in binary classification tasks. This makes
them suitable for classifying seizures, as seizures can be considered as one class and
all other types of brain activity can be considered as another class. SVM is a supervised
learning algorithm that needs the different classes to be labeled during the training pro-
cess. Based on the training data, the algorithm is able fit the best decision boundary for
class separation, which can then be used on unknown data. The general idea behind the
SVM algorithm is to determine an optimal hyperplane that has the ability to separate two
classes. The optimal hyperplane will result in the largest margin between the two classes.
2.7.1
Linearly separable data
For linearly separable data the goal of the SVM algorithm is to determine a hyperplane
defined by [19]:
−→
w · −→x + b = 0
(2.16)
Here −→x is a point or vector, −→
w is a vector normal to the hyperplane and b is an offset.
In supervised learning, −→x is accompanied by the label y, which either takes the value 1
18
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:33==
or -1 in binary classification. Additional hyperplanes that are parallel to the hyperplane in
Equation 2.16 and pass through objects on the border, which are known as the support
vectors, are defined as −→
w · −→x + b = 1 and −→
w · −→x + b = −1. The margin is the distance
between those two hyperplanes, which is shown in Figure 2.12. The distance can be
written as [19]:
D = |b1 −b2|

SVM, because the slack variables contribute heavily. For a very small C-value, the slack

2.8
Performance metrics
The classification of seizures versus non-seizures is based on labeled data in this thesis
and hence a supervised learning algorithm in the form of an SVM is applied. In supervised
learning, the confusion matrix is used to visualize the performance of the algorithm. The
confusion matrix for classification of seizures versus non-seizures is shown in Figure 2.17

[20]. The true negatives (TN) are classifications whose true and predicted classes are
non-seizures, whereas the true positives (TP) are classifications whose true and predicted
classes are seizures. The false negatives (FN) are seizures that are predicted as non-
seizures and the false positives (FP) are non-seizures that are predicted as seizures.

Based on the confusion matrix, different performance metrics can be obtained. Each
feature in the test data is labeled as either positive or negative in the binary classification
task, where positive corresponds to a seizure and negative corresponds to a non-seizure .
Here P will denote the amount positive features in the test data, whereas N will denote the
amount negative features in the test data. This means that the amount of real positives is
given by P and the amount of real negatives is given by N. The sensitivity or true positive
rate (TPR) is a measure of how well the classifier is able to detect seizures since only the
true positives are compared to the amount of real positives P. The sensitivity is defined
as:
sensitivity(TPR) =
TP
TP + FN = TP
P
(2.25)
The specificity or true negative rate (TNR) is a measure of how good the classifier is at not
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
23
==PAGE:38==
classifying non-seizure data as seizure data. Thus a high specificity indicates that there
are almost no false detections or alarms. The specificity is defined as:
specificity(TNR) =
TN
TN + FP = TN
N
(2.26)
EEG data generally has a large amount non-seizure features compared to seizure fea-
tures and therefore the classes are unbalanced. Because of this there will almost always
be a large amount true negatives and hence the specificity will almost always have a high
value. Another way to estimate if the classifier is good at avoiding false seizure detec-
tions is the false discovery rate (FDR), which will yield a low value when there are few
false seizure detections, whereas it will be high for a lot a false detections. The false
discovery rate is defined as:
FDR =
FP
FP + TP
(2.27)
Another metric that is used is the false alarm rate (FA), which is a measure of how many
false detections have been made on an hourly basis. The method applied in this thesis
performs classification every second and hence the total amount of classifications will
correspond to the total time. Based on this, the hourly false alarm rate can be defined as:
FA =
FP
TN + FP + TP + FN · 60 · 60 =
FP
N + P · 60 · 60
(2.28)
The last metric is the detection delay or latency, which is not based on the confusion
matrix. The delay is defined as the difference in seconds between the true seizure onset
OT and the predicted seizure onset OP , which is illustrated in Figure 2.18 and expressed
as:
delay = OT −OP
(2.29)

3
Related work
As mentioned in Section 2.5.2, the activity of the brain recorded by EEG can be charac-
terized by different variables, which are all associated with an individual’s age and state
of consciousness. Due to this, many seizure detection methods are designed to be used
for subject specific seizure detection, which the related work presented here is covering.
Seizure detection methods can be categorized as event detection methods and onset de-
tection methods. In event detection methods, the intention is to recognize a seizure to
its full extent with a minimal amount of errors. For onset detection methods, the goal is
to detect the start of a seizure with a minimal delay. Therefore, onset detection methods
only need a good accuracy at the start of a seizure and do not necessarily need a good
accuracy for the entire seizure event. In general event detection methods are used when
a seizure needs to be examined to its full extent, whereas onset detection methods are
used when a quick reaction to the seizure is needed. The focus of this thesis is aimed
at onset detection, because of the vast amount of data that is collected during long term
EEG recordings. In the clinical practice, evaluation of seizures is done by visual inspec-
tion, which is time consuming and often subjective. An automatic seizure onset detection
technique would therefore be able to indicate where the seizure starts, which could then
be validated by a professional. Furthermore, if the detection method provides trustworthy
results, it could be used in scenarios where a quick reaction is needed or as support for
visual analysis of EEG recordings.
As mentioned in the introduction of this thesis, the data set that is used is the CHB-MIT
Scalp EEG Database, which is further discussed in Chapter 4. Moreover, it was men-
tioned that the performance evaluation of the related works should be based on sensitiv-
ity, false alarm rate per hour and detection latency. The works presented in this chapter
are achieving the best results with regards to the specified conditions.
Application of machine learning to epileptic seizure onset detection and
treatment
Author/s: Ali Hossam Shoeb, Harvard-MIT Division of Health Sciences and Technology,
Massachusetts Institute of Technology, USA, 2009
The CHB-MIT data set was first used by Shoeb [4]. He presented a machine learning ap-
proach where patient-specific classifiers are constructed based on scalp EEG. Through
these classifiers, the onset of epileptic seizures can be detected. Shoeb’s approach cre-
ates feature vectors using a filter bank that computes the energy in different frequency
bands. The feature vectors are then concatenated to take both spatial and temporal prop-
erties into account. The feature vector is classified into seizure and non-seizure activity
using a support vector machine (SVM) in combination with a radial basis function (RBF)
kernel. The method achieves a sensitivity of 96% with an average detection delay of 4.6
seconds and a median false alarm rate of 0.07 per hour.
Real-time epileptic seizure detection using EEG
Author/s: Lasitha S. Vidyaratne and Khan M. Iftekharuddin, Vision Lab, Department of
Electrical and Computer Engineering, Old Dominion University, Norfolk, USA, 2017
A more recent study by Vidyaratne and Iftekharuddin [21] makes use of a wavelet decom-
position method, known as the harmonic wavelet packet transform (HWPT), and fractal
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
25
==PAGE:40==
dimension (FD) estimation. The feature vectors are organized in a similar way as de-
scribed by Shoeb, where spectral, fractal, spatial, and temporal information from EEG
is encoded. Classification is performed by a relevance vector machine (RVM). The pro-
posed algorithm achieves a sensitivity of 96% with an average detection delay of 1.89
seconds and a median false alarm rate of 0.1 per hour.
Seizure onset detection based on detection of changes in brain activity
quantified by evolutionary game theory model
Author/s: Ramtin Hamavar and Babak Mohammadzadeh Asl, Department of Electrical
and Computer Engineering, Tarbiat Modares University, Tehran, Iran, 2021
Another study by Hamavar and Asl [22] proposes a method where the brain activity is mod-
eled using evolutionary game theory and Kalman filtering. The model estimates values
that are compared to measured values. Once the difference between the measurement
and the estimate becomes too large a seizure onset is declared using a change point
detector. This method achieves a sensitivity of 100% with an average detection delay
of -0.8 seconds and an average false alarm rate of 0.39 per hour. The negative value
of the delay indicates that the method is able to detect seizures before they begin. This
however has to be interpreted with caution as the reported value for delay is an average.
They state that the framework is able to detect the signs of onset from the change in
brain-describing relationships long before the onset of seizures due to changes in many
linear and non-linear features of EEG.
Comparison of methods
A comparison of the different methods shows a trade-off between the performance met-
rics. In terms of sensitivity, the two first methods are identical, whereas the third method
is superior. In terms of average detection delay, the method proposed by Hamavar and
Asl achieves the best detection delay but there is a slight trade-off in false alarm rate per
hour. The two first methods both approximately yield 2 false detections over a period of
24 hours, whereas the last method approximately yields 9 false detections over a period
of 24 hours. The results of the discussed methods are summarized in Table 3.1.
Table 3.1: Summary of results obtained in related works.
Method
Sensitivity
Delay (s)
False alarm rate
Shoeb (2009)
96 %
3 (median)
4.6 (mean)
0.07/h (median)
0.13/h (mean)
Vidyaratne and Iftekharuddin (2017
96 %
1.34 (median)
1.89 (mean)
0.1/h (median)
Hamavar and Asl (2021)
100 %
-0.80 (mean)
0.39/h (mean)
The methods proposed by Shoeb and Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin are used as a foundation
for this project, implying that elements of both publications are used. Even though the
performance obtained by Hamavar & Asl is superior in terms sensitivity and delay, the
trade-off in false alarm rate is quite significant. The results of the three methods will be
used to evaluate the results of the method that is implemented in this project.

4
Methods
This chapter provides a description of the data and the methods applied to detect the
onset of seizures. This includes the process of data preparation, feature extraction, clas-
sification and the implementation of an algorithm that is able to detect seizures in real
time.
4.1
Data
The data used throughout this work is the CHB-MIT Scalp EEG Database [4][5]. This
database was collected at the Children’s Hospital Boston and consists of EEG recordings
from pediatric subjects with intractable seizures. The recordings were collected from 22
subjects grouped into 23 cases, where 5 subjects are males of age 3-22 years and 17
subjects are females of age 1.5-19 years. Case 1 and 21 are collected from the same
subject with a time difference of 1.5 years. Case 24 was added to the collection in Decem-
ber 2010 and has no subject information, so it is unknown whether this case is collected
from one of the 22 subjects or from a new subject. All of the cases contain between 9 and
42 continuous EEG recordings and the signals are sampled at 256 Hz. Table 4.1 shows
the subject information for the different cases.

4.1.1
Data preparation
The data is publicly available at [5], and it was further prepared for processing by the
Department of Health Technology at DTU, such that the recordings containing seizures
were labeled with easily accessible starting and ending times. The methods described
by Shoeb [4] and Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin [21] utilize seizures with a duration of 20
seconds. Furthermore the algorithm proposed by Shoeb is trained on 2 or more seizures
per case whereas Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin follow a leave-one-out scheme. Some of
the cases consist of only 3 seizures and 30 of the seizures last less than 20 seconds. Both
methods make use of seizures lasting for at least 20 seconds and therefore not all seizures
will be considered and even some cases will be excluded. The approach used here follows
the method proposed by Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin to avoid the problem of not having
enough seizures to train the algorithm. This implies that only seizures lasting at least 20
seconds are considered and that eventually a leave-one-out scheme is applied. Table
4.3 shows the different cases, where additional columns have been added to indicate the
amount of seizures lasting at least 20 seconds and their respective durations. In the table
it can be seen that case 16 only has seizures lasting less than 20 seconds and hence
this case is omitted. Furthermore case 24 only has EEG recordings containing seizures,
which means that not enough non-seizure data is available. This case is also omitted.
Other than omitting certain seizures and cases, no pre-proccesing of the data has been
performed.
4.1.2
Data extraction
The first step of extracting seizure and non-seizure data from the recordings was to ex-
tract every seizure event from every case resulting in a total of 185 seizures, which was
narrowed down to 143 after omitting the aforementioned seizures and cases. This was
done to separate seizure data from non-seizure data. Furthermore at least 12 hours of
non-seizure data was extracted for every case, except for case 12, where only 11 hours
of non-seizure data was available. It is important to emphasize that seizure and non-
seizure data were separated at all times during the process of implementing the algorithm
for seizure detection. This also means that non-seizure data from recordings containing
seizures was omitted. The seizure and non-seizure data was saved such that it could be
applied for feature extraction. The code used for extracting seizure events is attached in
Appendix A.1 and the code used for extracting non-seizure events is attached in Appendix
A.2.
4.2
Feature extraction
The feature extraction process involves both the methods proposed by Shoeb and Vid-
yaratne & Iftekharuddin. To be more specific, the features are extracted based on the
harmonic wavelet packet transform, which was applied by Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin and
the way the feature vectors are structured is based on the method proposed by Shoeb.
Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin structure their feature vectors in a similar way as Shoeb.
4.2.1
Computation of features
The implementation of the harmonic wavelet packet transform follows Equation 2.11 from
Section 2.6.4, which is repeated here for convenience:
HWT(m, n, f) = X(f)Ψ∗((n −m)f)
Here X(f) is the Fourier transform of the signal x(t) and Ψ∗((n −m)f) is the complex
conjugate of the Fourier transform of the harmonic wavelet at scale (m, n). The imple-

mentation of the harmonic wavelet transform can be performed as shown in Figure 4.3
[18]. This implementation utilizes the Fourier transform.
To implement the harmonic wavelet packet transform, the scale parameters m and n need
to be specified based on n −m = fb. In Section 2.6.5 it was stated that the bandwidth of
the harmonic wavelet is based on the highest frequency component fh of the signal x(t)
and the decomposition level j:
fb = fh
2j
The EEG signals considered here are sampled at a frequency of 256 Hz, which means
that the highest frequency component is 256
2 = 128 Hz according to the Nyquist frequency.
The decomposition level proposed by Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin is j = 5, such that the
bandwidth of the harmonic wavelet at decomposition level j = 5 results in:
fb = 128
25 = 4 Hz

Figure 4.3: Implementation of the harmonic wavelet transform [18]. The input signal x(t)
is first Fourier transformed and then multiplied with the harmonic wavelet at scale m, n in
the frequency domain resulting in frequency domain wavelet coefficients. The coefficients
are then inverse Fourier transformed resulting in time domain wavelet coefficients.
This means that the scale parameters will be:
m = l · 4,
l = 0, 1, ..., 2j −1
n = (l + 1) · 4,
l = 0, 1, ..., 2j −1
The result of this is that the harmonic wavelet packet coefficients each will cover a band-
width of 4 Hz ranging from 0 to 128 Hz. So in total there will be 25 = 32 groups of
coefficients. Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin proposed the frequency range from 0 to 32 Hz,
whereas Shoeb proposed the frequency range from 0.5 to 24.5 Hz. The implementation
here also uses the frequency range from 0 to 32 Hz, because of the specified bandwidth.
This implies that only the first 8 groups of coefficients covering the frequency range from
0 to 32 Hz are used to create the feature vectors. The feature vectors are based on the
energy contained in each 4 Hz frequency band of the decomposition. The energy is de-
fined as the sum of the squared coefficients of the lth subband in the decomposition level
j [21]:
E(j, l) =
N
∑
k=1
|hwt(m, n, k)|2 =
N
∑
k=1
|hwpt(j, l, k)|2
(4.1)
The code used to compute the harmonic wavelet packet coefficients and the energy spec-
ified by Equation 4.1 is attached in Appendix A.3.

4.2.2
Feature vector structure
The feature vectors are created based on the signals from single EEG channels. Each
signal is analyzed in epochs of duration L = 2 seconds. An epoch is then used in the
harmonic wavelet packet transform, where M = 8 subband energies are calculated. This
process is illustrated in Figure 4.4.

Figure 4.4: Spectral feature extraction method [4]. An epoch of size L from EEG channel
k is passed to the wavelet packet transform spanning the range 0-32 Hz. The energy in
each band Sm is computed to form the feature vector x consisting of M elements.
To include the spatial content of the EEG recordings, all K = 18 EEG channels are ana-
lyzed using the method mentioned above. For each epoch, M energies are calculated in
each channel. This implies that M ·K features are computed for each epoch. The energy
features are concatenated to include the spatial content. This process is shown in Figure
4.5.

XT
Figure 4.5: Spatial feature extraction method [4]. The feature vectors x from each channel
are concatenated to form the feature vector XT of size M · K for an epoch of size L.
The feature vector XT includes both spectral and spatial components. It however does
not capture the time evolution. This implies that the current epoch will not be able to con-
vey information about previous epochs. Therefore, the last step of creating the feature
vectors involves the inclusion of time evolution. The time evolution is included by con-
catenating W feature vectors XT , that do not overlap. This is done as shown in Equation
4.2.
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
33
==PAGE:48==
χT = [XT−(W−1)L ... XT−2L XT−L XT ]
(4.2)
Here W is the amount of feature vectors XT concatenated to form the feature vector
χT . Based on the methods proposed in related works, W is set to W = 3, so three
feature vectors XT are concatenated to form a single feature vector χT that includes
time evolution. Thus Equation 4.2 becomes:
χT = [XT−2L XT−L XT ]
The epoch, which serves as a window, is moved across the signal with a step size of
1 second, such that there is an overlap of 1 second for each window. This is done to
simulate real time detection. An illustration of this process is depicted in Figure 4.6. A
single resulting feature vector χT that is used for classification is of dimension M ·K ·W =
8·18·3 = 432. For seizure recordings, the process is repeated until 20 seconds of a seizure
have been processed. For non-seizure recordings, the process is repeated until 12 hours
have been processed for all of the cases, except for case 12, where only 11 hours of
non-seizure data are available. 12 hours of non-seizure data is used to attempt to cover
the many different characteristics of brain activity throughout the day. Seizure activity is
sparse compared to normal activity and therefore the two classes will be imbalanced.

Figure 4.6: Inclusion of time evolution for the feature vectors [4]. Three non-overlapping
feature vectors XT are concatenated to form the feature vector χT of size M ·K ·W. The
first feature vector χT is formed after 6 seconds and after that a new one is formed every
second.
The parameters used to create the feature vectors are summarized in Table 4.4. These
parameters were chosen based on the related work by Shoeb and Vidyaratne & Iftekharud-
din.

The feature vectors for both seizure data and non-seizure data were stored, such that they
can be applied to train the classifiers. An example of code used to extract the features for
a single case, which can be modified to include other cases, is attached in Appendix A.4.

Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:49==
4.3
Classification
The classification process involves training a subject specific classifier for each seizure
based on a leave-one-out scheme. After the training process, the classifier is tested
on the EEG recording containing the left out seizure. The testing process also includes
computation of performance metrics.
4.3.1
Training
The training of subject specific classifiers for each seizure is based on a leave-one-out
scheme. For the first case, where seven seizures are available, seven classifiers are
trained, where for each classifier six seizures are used as training data and the last one
is left for testing. The trained classifier is then only intended for the seizure that is left out.
The non-seizure training data is used for all seven seizures, since no testing is performed
on the non-seizure data. This training process is used for all cases. The classifier training
is performed using a Gaussian SVM implemented in MATLAB’s Classification Learner
app, which is a part of the Statistics and Machine Learning Toolbox [23]. The function
used to train the classifiers is attached in Appendix A.5 and a script that trains several
models for a single case is attached in Appendix A.6. Initially a linear kernel was used
for the SVM, which yielded good results for the first case. For the other cases, the linear
kernel resulted in a lot of false seizure detections. After applying the Gaussian kernel,
the overall accuracy was reduced, but the onsets of most of the seizures were able to
be detected without a lot of false detections. To implement the SVM classifier, the box
constraint parameter C needs to be specified. As mentioned in the background chapter,
this parameter determines how much misclassifications contribute to the optimization.
This value was set to C = 1. In MATLAB the kernel scale is a parameter that divides all
elements of the predictor matrix by the value of the kernel scale. This parameter was set
to auto, such that the software finds the best value based on a heuristic procedure.
4.3.2
Testing
The trained classifiers are used on seizure recordings to test if the seizures are able to
be detected. An EEG recording containing the left out seizure is used. The recording is
analyzed by a 2 second window that calculates the energy in the specific frequency bands
in each channel, which as mentioned earlier are concatenated. The window is then moved
1 second and new energy features are calculated for each channel. The first classification
is performed after 6 seconds, where the first feature vector χT is formed. This is due to
the parameter W = 3. The window is then again moved 1 second and a new classification
is performed. After reaching the 6 second mark, classification is performed every second.
This process continues until the window reaches the end of the signal. Figure 4.7 shows
the architecture of the seizure detector used during testing [4]. This testing method results
in a binary vector, where seizures are indicated as 1 and other brain activity is indicated
as 0. During the first 5 seconds no classification is performed and therefore the first 5
elements of the output vector are initialized as zeros. This is done to keep the correct
duration of the EEG recording. Once the EEG recording has been completely processed,
the output vector can be compared to the true label to compute the confusion matrix and
the relevant performance metrics. The true label is based on the annotations in the data.
Examples of code used to perform the testing procedure are attached in Appendices A.7
and A.8.
4.3.3
Performance
The performance metrics are computed based on the output of the SVM classifier. The
sensitivity, specificity, false discovery rate and false alarm rate per hour are all computed
in terms of correctly classified feature vectors, whereas the latency is computed based
on the first detection after the true onset. As an example the first seizure from case 1

Here it can be seen that the sensitivity only achieves a value of 0.73. This however is
not truly representative in terms of onset detection since the seizure is correctly detected.
Therefore, the sensitivity can also be determined in terms of correctly detected seizures.
In this case the detector is able to correctly detect the seizure, which is then regarded as
one out of one seizures correctly detected resulting in a sensitivity of 1.
The specificity is calculated as:
specificity = TN
N
= 3559
3559 = 1
This indicates that there are no false seizure detections, which is also indicated by the
false discovery rate:
FDR =
FP
FP + TP =
0
0 + 30 = 0
The specificity and false discovery rate are similar metrics as can be seen from the two
achieved values. Generally the specificity will be high because of the large amount of true
negatives that are present in EEG data, in the form of normal brain activity, which is why
the false discovery has been included. In recordings where multiple seizures are present,
the specificity and false discovery rate will be affected, because the seizures in those
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
37
==PAGE:52==
recordings have not been separated. This implies that the classifier performs classification
partly on training data, which is not ideal because it will affect both of the metrics. The
results obtained from testing on EEG recordings that contain multiple seizures are further
discussed in Chapter 5.
For seizures that are not detected, the delay is declared as a NaN value. For cases
where some seizures are not detected, the average delay is only calculated based on the
seizures that are detected. If a case for example contains five seizures and only three
seizures are detected, the average of the detection delay is only calculated based on the
three seizures that are detected and the seizures that are not detected are disregarded
in the calculation. The same is done for the false discovery rate, because this metric will
result in division by zero if no seizure is detected.
As classification is performed every second, the sum of all elements in the confusion
matrix corresponds to the total time of the EEG recording that is being processed. The
false alarm rate per hour is thus computed as:
FA =
FP
TN + FP + TP + FN · 60 · 60 =
0
3559 + 11 + 30 · 60 · 60 = 0
In recordings where multiple seizures are present, the false alarm rate will also be af-
fected. The false detections that occur, because there are multiple seizures present in
the recording, are partly disregarded for this metric. This is done because the false alarm
rate per hour is used for comparison with the results obtained in related works.

Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:53==
5
Results
This chapter presents the results obtained in the individual cases in terms of sensitivity,
specificity, false discovery rate, delay and hourly false alarm rate. The reported values
are based on single seizures accompanied by an average of all the seizures in a single
case. Furthermore the sensitivity, delay and hourly false alarm rate across all seizures
and cases are reported. Lastly a comparison with the results obtained in related works is
made.
5.1
Subject specific results
The subject specific results are shown in the following tables. The sensitivity, specificity,
false discovery rate and hourly false alarm rate are all computed in terms of correctly clas-
sified feature vectors, whereas the delay is computed based on the difference between
the true onset and the detected onset. Furthermore, the sensitivity is also reported in
terms of correctly detected seizures. Cases that contain recordings with multiple seizures
are cases 4, 6, 9, 12, 13, 14, 15, 20 and 23. In those cases the false discovery rate is
affected by the fact that multiple seizure are present in the recording. For example in case
4, seizures 3 and 4 are part of the same recording and therefore the false discovery rate
is as high as 0.55. This is caused because the seizures in the recording have not been
separated. The sensitivity and delay however are not affected by this. Furthermore, the
hourly false alarm rate is also affected if multiple seizures are present in one recording.
The hourly false alarm rate is to be compared to results in related works and therefore
the hourly false alarm rate is both computed in terms of regarding the other seizures as
false detections but also without regarding them as false detections. In the latter case,
the values are indicated in brackets. The only case where no seizures are able to be
detected is case 17. An attempt was made to increase the amount of non-seizure training
data from 12 hours to 17 hours in case 17. The result of this was that one seizure was
able to be detected, whereas the other two still remained undetected. Confusion matrices
and detection plots are attached in Appendices B.1 to B.22.

5.2
Results across all seizures and cases
In total, the testing has been performed on 143 seizures out of which 129 have been
correctly detected. This implies that 14 seizures were not detected. Based on this, the
resulting sensitivity in terms of correctly detected seizures is 129
143 = 0.9. The delay is com-
puted as the average of all delays, which results in a value of 7.64 seconds. In cases
where recordings contain multiple seizures, the hourly false alarm rate has been com-
puted, without regarding the other seizures as false detections. This resulted in a value of
11 false alarms per hour across all seizures and cases. The average sensitivity in terms
of correctly detected seizures, the average delay and the average hourly false alarm rate
are summarized in Table 5.24. The specificity and the false discovery rate have not been
included, because these metrics do not convey a lot of information in terms real time per-
formance. As can be seen from the results, the overall performance is worse than the
performances achieved in related works. A comparison of the methods including this one
is shown in Table 5.25, where additional median values have been added for the delay
and false alarm rate. The median value of the delay is only based on the seizures that
have been detected, which implies that the NaN values are disregarded. The difference
in sensitivity is 6% when compared to Shoeb and Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin, whereas
the difference is 10% when compared to the method proposed by Hamavar & Asl. In
terms of average and median detection delay, the method presented here is slower than
the other methods. The most noticeable difference when comparing the results, is the
average false alarm rate per hour, which is considerably larger than the average false
alarm rate obtained in the other works. The median false alarm rate, on the other hand,
is lower, indicating that the many false detections mainly originate from specific cases.
