==PAGE:61==
6
Discussion
A major goal of this thesis was to implement a subject specific seizure onset detection
method to prove that the principle behind seizure detection can be accomplished. The
feasibility of a seizure detector as a potential device that offers assistance in the healthcare
sector is to be determined based on the findings. As the focus is centered on onset
detection, the major performance metrics are the sensitivity, the detection delay and the
false alarm rate per hour. The results obtained with regard to these metrics across all
seizures and cases are a sensitivity of 90%, an average detection delay of 7.6 seconds,
a median detection delay of 6 seconds, an average false alarm rate of 11.21 per hour and
a median false alarm rate of 0 per hour.
The overall performance is worse than the performances achieved in related works in
terms of sensitivity, detection delay and false alarm rate per hour. The bad performance
in average false alarm rate is mostly caused by specific cases, such as case 3, where
a great number of false detections are observed. This is also indicated by the median
false alarm rate. The median false alarm rate shows that the performance of the detector
is largely dependent on the specific case and that certain cases influence the average
performance greatly. This indicates that the detector may have to be tuned to match the
specific patient.
The overall sensitivity is 90%, which is not as good as the sensitivities presented in related
works. This is most likely due to the extraction process of the non-seizure data. The
exact EEG recordings used for training are not specified in the related works, which likely
influences the result. Furthermore, the number of hours of non-seizure data used to train
the classifiers varies in the different works. The method presented here used 12 hours
of non-seizure data for all but one of the cases, whereas Shoeb [4] used 24 hours and
Vidyaratne & Iftekharuddin [21] used 10 hours. In some cases only a limited amount is
available, which can lead to that the normal brain activity is not covered to full extent when
training the classifier. For example, if only non-seizure data, that is recorded during the
night, is available, the activity of the brain during the day is not covered. It is likely that
the daily activity might be classified falsely due to this, resulting in a bad detector that
produces a lot of false alarms. An investigation of how much data should be used to train
the classifiers was not conducted, which makes it difficult to exactly conclude why the
performance of the implemented method achieves worse results. The only case where
the amount of non-seizure data was increased, was in case 17. This was done because no
seizures were detected. Through the use of additional non-seizure data, a single seizure
was detected, which indicates that additional non-seizure data perhaps can increase the
performance in terms of sensitivity. This should however be further investigated, as it is
difficult to draw any conclusions based on a single case.
In cases where the same number of seizures is available for training, the results are not
necessarily comparable. This is for example seen in cases 2 and 6. In case 2 both
seizures are able to be detected, whereas only one seizure is able to be detected in case
6. In cases containing only three seizures, the general trend is that the use of features
extracted from only two seizures is enough to detect the onset of an unseen seizure. The
only case where this does not apply is case 17. The other cases 7, 11, 19, 21 and 22
achieve an acceptable performance in terms of sensitivity and delay.
The achieved delay is also not as good as the delay presented in related works. A way
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
47
==PAGE:62==
to improve the delay could be to extract an additional few seconds of non-seizure data
before each seizure and use the features extracted from this for training. The result of this
is that the transition from non-seizure data to seizure data will be encoded in the feature
vectors and eventually learned by the classifier instead of just the onset of the seizure.
This could perhaps help the detector to determine the seizure earlier. Additionally, through
the use of this method, the seizures lasting less than 20 seconds could be included in the
whole process.
Another observation is that the detectors are able to perform rather well, given that the
data is highly unbalanced during the training process, which is caused by the large amount
of normal EEG data. This indicates that the SVM-algorithm is able to handle unbalanced
data.
In terms of overall sensitivity, the implemented method is able to detect approximately
90% of all the seizures that were used for testing. This shows that seizure detectors
potentially can be used as devices that offer assistance for visual inspection. The perfor-
mance however is not good enough to be completely relied upon, which means that this
specific seizure detection method cannot replace a professional, that carries out the task
of visual inspection off EEG recordings.
With regard to overall average and median delay, the implemented method is able to
detect seizures within the time range of 6 to 7.6 seconds. For certain types of seizures
that have a long duration, this amount of detection delay will not play a large role as the
rest of the seizure can be attended to. For seizures that only last a short duration, the
amount of delay is too large. In cases where short seizures occur, the clinicians or other
affected people will not have enough time to react to the seizure if no detection is made
during the first 6 to 7 seconds. Furthermore, the detector is not well suited for situations
where an immediate reaction to a seizure is needed.
For the average false alarm rate per hour, the detector detects too many false seizures.
11 false detections per hour are simply too many false alarms to deal with, which most
likely will result in that the detections will be ignored in the long run. The median false
alarm rate per hour, on the other hand, shows that the detection method might be suitable
for certain individuals, whereas it needs to be tuned for other individuals.
The overall outcome of the presented method is that the principle of seizure detection is
feasible even though the detector presented here does not achieve optimal results. To
further improve the method, an investigation of different parameters should be made. This
includes an investigation of how much data should be used for training and also which
parameters should be used during the process of designing and structuring the feature
vectors. The selection of frequency bands can also be further investigated to determine
the optimal frequency range for seizure detection. Furthermore, the EEG recordings con-
taining multiple seizures should be separated, just to ease the testing process.
48
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:63==
7
Conclusion
The primary objective of this thesis was to investigate methods for epileptic seizure de-
tection based on scalp EEG. This included the process of showing proof of principle by
implementing a method from the literature and based on the findings, determine the feasi-
bility of a seizure detector. To successfully achieve this, several sub-goals were defined.
The first sub-goal was to perform a literature study of epileptic seizure detection methods
based on EEG technology. Through the literature study, methods were analyzed and
investigated. This provided an overview and understanding of the research field. From
the analysis, it was decided to focus on subject specific onset detection and the CHB-MIT
Scalp EEG Database [4][5].
As the publicly available data of interest had been used in many of the relevant publi-
cations, it could already be concluded in the literature study that the data was of high
quality. Nonetheless, the data of interest was investigated and analyzed. The investi-
gation proved what was stated in the literature. The data contained plenty of seizures
and enough normal EEG data to be used for seizure detection. The analysis determined
which cases, seizures and EEG channels in the data could be used.
The third sub-goal was to replicate a method from the literature as proof-of-principle. An
exact replication of a single method was not implemented, but elements from the meth-
ods for subject specific seizure onset detection proposed by Shoeb [4] and Vidyaratne &
Iftekharuddin [21] were used.
The implemented method was evaluated based on real time performance. This was done
through a testing process where the evaluation of the method was based on sensitivity,
detection delay and hourly false alarm rate across all of the selected cases and seizures.
The performance was then compared to the performance obtained in related works. This
comparison showed that the implemented detection method was inferior to the methods
proposed in related works. Nonetheless, the implemented method was able to detect
90% of the selected seizures in real time, which proved the principle of the two methods
that were used as a foundation.
In conclusion, the findings of this thesis show that subject specific seizure onset detection
based on EEG signals is possible. Furthermore, the findings show that seizure detection
methods, through further improvement, potentially can be used to support the healthcare
sector. This especially concerns visual inspection of EEG recordings and scenarios where
a quick reaction to a seizure is needed.
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
49
==PAGE:64==
50
Automatic Epileptic Seizure Detection based on scalp EEG and machine learning
==PAGE:65==
8
Future work
As mentioned in the discussion and conclusion, improvements to the presented seizure
detection method can be made. This chapter covers potential improvements that can be
considered for the implemented method.
Investigation of frequency bands: The frequency bands used for energy extraction
were selected through the recommendations found in the literature. A thorough investi-
gation of the optimal frequency range that best characterizes seizure activity could poten-
tially improve the implemented method.
Investigation of feature vector parameters: The feature vector parameters specified
by the number of subbands M, the number of EEG channels K and the number of feature
vectors W concatenated to form the final feature vector χT were also selected through the
recommendations found in the literature. An investigation of these parameters could be
conducted to determine a learning rate that results in optimal feature vectors for seizure
detection.
Investigation of data parameters: The data parameters are the number of hours of non-
seizure data used to extract the feature vectors for the non-seizure class and number of
seconds of seizure data used to extract the feature vectors for the seizure class. For one
of the cases in the database, it was seen that an increase in non-seizure data resulted in a
higher sensitivity. This indicates that an investigation of these parameters could potentially
increase the performance of the method by determining a learning rate.
Non-subject specific seizure onset detection: Subject specific seizure detection re-
quires EEG data to be collected for every subject, which can be inconvenient in certain
scenarios. A generalized method that could be used as a plug and play system could
therefore be beneficial. This might be challenging through the use of EEG only, since the
electrographic characteristics of seizures vary from individual to individual. Nonetheless, it
could be interesting to investigate if the implemented method can be used for non-subject
specific seizure onset detection.

A.3
Harmonic wavelet packet transform function
This function is used to compute the harmonic wavelet packet coefficients and the energy
in each subband.
1
function [hwt,Energy]=hwpt(x,fs)
2
% x - input signal
3
% fs - sampling frequency
4
5
x=x-mean(x);
6
s=5; % decompostion level
7
8
xsize=size(x);
9
if xsize(2)==1
10
x=x';
11
end
12
L=length(x);
13
X=fft(x,L);
14
df=fs/L; %scale with length
15
16
17
fh=fs/2;
18
fb=fh/(2^s);
19
20
k=2^s; % index of coefficient , not used here
21
22
i=0:2^s-1; % index of sub-band
23
24
m=i*fb; % scale parameters
25
n=(i+1)*fb;
26
27
% for h = 1:k % use this if all 2^s groups of coefficients are to be used
28
for h = 1:8 % only using the first 8 groups of coefficients corresponding to
0-32 Hz
29
W=zeros(1,L);
30
for c = floor(m(h)/df)+1:floor(n(h)/df)+1
31
W(c) = 1;
32
%W(c) = 1/fb;
33
end
34
HWT(h,:)=X.*conj(W);% multiplication in frequency domain
35
hwt(h,:)=ifft(HWT(h,:),L);
36
Energy(h)=sum(abs(hwt(h,:)).^2); % compute energy
37
end
38
end

A.4
Feature extraction
This code is used to extract features from both seizure data and non-seizure data. The
case is specified by chb01 in this example, which can be changed to the other cases
ranging from chb01-chb24. Furthermore the feature vector chi_ and and chi_non_ also
need the correct case number to be assigned.
1
%% Paths
2
addpath(genpath('C:\Users\hoedt\OneDrive\Documents\Medicin og Teknologi DTU\5.
semester\kandidatspeciale '));
3
4
%% Load
5
load('chb01_seizures.mat') % load stored seizures
6
%% init
7
fs=256;
8
K = 18;
9
10
%% Feature extraction for seizure
11
% chb01_seizures(seizure_to_be_excluded)=[]; % exclude seizure if shorter than
20 s
12
E=cell(1,18);
13
sz_vector=cell(1,19);
14
chi_01=cell(length(chb01_seizures),15);
15
16
for j = 1:length(chb01_seizures) %number of seizures to extract features from
17
for p = 0:18 % 20s into the seizure
18
for k = 1:K % loop through channels
19
[~,E{k}] = hwpt(chb01_seizures{j}(1+fs*p:fs*(2+p),k)',fs); %
compute energy
20
end
21
sz_vector{p+1}=cell2mat(E);
22
end
23
for d = 1:length(sz_vector)-4 % creating chi vector
24
chi_01{j,d} = [sz_vector{d} sz_vector{d+2} sz_vector{d+4}];
25
end
26
end
27
28
%save('chi_01.mat', 'chi_01', '-v7.3')
29
30
%% Feature extraction for non-seizure
31
load('chb01_non_seizures.mat') % load stored non-seizure data
32
E_nsz=cell(1,18);
33
34
for j = 1:size(chb01_non_seizures ,2) %number of hours to extract features from
35
for p = 0:length(chb01_non_seizures{j})/fs-2 %for window movement
36
for k = 1:K % loop through channels
37
[~,E_nsz{k}] = hwpt(chb01_non_seizures{j}(1+fs*p:fs*(2+p),k)',fs);
% compute energy
38
end
39
non_sz_vector{p+1}=cell2mat(E_nsz);
40
end
41
for d=1:length(non_sz_vector)-4 % creating chi vector
42
chi_non_01{j,d} = [non_sz_vector{d} non_sz_vector{d+2} non_sz_vector{d
+4}];
43
end
44
end
45

the same order and format as your training \ndata. Do not include the
response column or any columns you did not import into the app. \n \nFor
more information , see <a href="matlab:helpview(fullfile(docroot , ''stats
'', ''stats.map''), ''appclassification_exportmodeltoworkspace '')">How to

A.6
Classifier training script
This code is used to train a model for every seizure for a single case. In this particular
example case 13 is used, which can be changed to any other case. The model name
Mdl_ should also be changed in accordance with the case.
1
%% Paths
2
addpath(genpath('C:\Users\hoedt\OneDrive\Documents\Medicin og Teknologi DTU\5.
semester\kandidatspeciale '));
3
%% Load stored seizure and non-seizure features
4
load('chi_non_13.mat');
5
load('chi_13.mat');
6
7
%% Create train data for each seizure
8
train_data_non = chi_non_13(1:12,:); % use only 12 hours of non-seizure data
9
train_data=chi_13;
10
11
%% Create predictors and labels
12
X_non = cell2mat(reshape(train_data_non ',[length(train_data_non(:,1))*length(
train_data_non),1]));
13
14
for i = 1:length(train_data(:,1))
15
clear label
16
17
temp=train_data;
18
temp(i,:)=[]; % exclude current seizure for model training
19
20
X = cell2mat(reshape(temp ',[],1));
21
X_comb = [X; X_non];
22
X_train{i}=X_comb;
23
24
for d = 1:length(X(:,1))+length(X_non(:,1))
25
if d <= length(X(:,1))
26
label(d) = 1; %seizure=1
27
else
28
label(d) = 0; %non-seizure=0
29
end
30
end
31
label=logical(label);
32
y{i}=label;
33
end
34
35
%% Train classifier
36
for i = 1:length(train_data(:,1))
37
[Mdl_13{i}, ~] = trainClassifier2([X_train{i} y{i}']);
38
end
39
40
save('-v7.3','Mdl_13.mat','Mdl_13 ')

A.7
Classifier testing script for recordings containing only
one seizure
This code is used to test a model for every seizure for a single case, where recordings do
not contain multiple seizures. In this particular example case 19 is used. For other cases,
the corresponding seizure recordings have to be loaded and the variable names need to
be changed. Furthermore if a recording contains multiple seizures the script needs further
modification. An example of this is shown in Appendix A.8.
1
%% Paths
2
addpath(genpath('C:\Users\hoedt\OneDrive\Documents\Medicin og Teknologi DTU\5.
semester\kandidatspeciale '));

addpath(genpath('C:\Users\hoedt\Desktop\extractedEEGAndSeizures '))
4
5
fs=256;
6
K=18;

32

33

35

36

37

38

39

40

41

43

45

,1]));

48
if d >= fileMeta_test{i}.seizureStartSecond1 && d <= fileMeta_test{i}.
seizureEndSecond1
49
label_test(d) = 1;

yfit{i} = [zeros(5,1); Mdl_19{i}.predictFcn(X_test{i})];

A.8
Classifier testing script for recordings containing
multiple seizures
This code is used to test a model for every seizure for a single case, where some record-
ings contain multiple seizures. In this particular example case 13 is used. For other cases,
the corresponding seizure recordings have to be loaded and the variable names need to
be changed. Furthermore the indices of the seizure recordings have to be modified when
generating the true labels and computing the delay.
1
